class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Coo&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CoO">CoO</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Open+Framework+Carbon-sphere&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_open%20framework%20carbon-sphere">open framework carbon-sphere</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Heterojunctions&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_heterojunctions">heterojunctions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Dual+Carbon+Strategy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_dual%20carbon%20strategy">dual carbon strategy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion battery (SIB), as a new energy storage system, has merits of low cost and abundant reserves and, thus, has been regarded as an ideal candidate to replace the commonly used lithium-ion battery (LIB) for large-scale energy-storage systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, the SIBs have many issues to be solved before their commercial applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Among these, the electrode material becomes the dominant factor to limit its electrochemical performance in practical applications. Although the chemical properties of sodium (Na) are very similar to those of lithium (Li), Na<sup>+</sup> ions (1.02 Å) have a larger radius than Li<sup>+</sup> ions (0.76 Å), which reduces the ionic migration efficiency and intensifies pulverization during cycling of the SIB. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> Therefore, there is a critical demand to search for the anode material for the SIB with a high reversible capacity, fast reaction kinetics, and good structural stability. The anode materials for the SIB can be classified into three major categories according to their reaction mechanisms, i.e., the insertion type, the conversion type, and the alloying type. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> They all have various problems, e.g., the low theoretical capacity for the insertion type, severe volume expansion, and poor cyclic stability for the alloying type and conversion type. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> A common solution for these issues is to combine high-capacity but poor-cyclability materials with the conductive and stable skeletons to comprehensively improve the overall electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Transition metal chalcogenides (TMCs), especially Co-based materials, have high theoretical capacities, mainly attributed to their multielectron reaction abilities with Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 15 to 19">(15−19)</a> For example, mesoporous Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se nanocrystals present a capacity retention of 88% after 3000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> (212.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> CoS<sub>2</sub> showed a reversible discharge capacity of 724 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and could be stabilized by combining with multiple wall carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Nanostructured Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> delivered a reversible capacity of 447 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 50 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> FeS<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se<sub><i>x</i></sub> combines the advantages of FeS<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub> and achieves high stability and high-rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> However, there are few studies on using CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO heterojunctions for the electrodes of a SIB. Porous heterojunction nanoparticles are effective to increase the electrical conductivity of metal sulfides, provide more active sites for the conversion of Na<sup>+</sup> as well as the decomposition of irreversible SEI layers, and increase the atomic utilization, thus improving the sodium storage capacity of the electrode materials. On the other hand, many carbon materials have been explored as the skeletons of SIBs’ anode materials, such as hard carbon, graphene, and carbon nanofibers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> For example, a VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO composite with a rGO skeleton produced a discharge capacity of ∼241 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> CoPSe@layered N-doped carbon prepared by layered zeolitic imidazolate frameworks (ZIFs) achieved a capacity of 303 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 500 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> As one type of metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), ZIFs can be used as the precursor to synthesize a variety of porous materials. Although these carbon skeletons play positive roles in enhancing the dispersion, conductivity, and capacity of the composites,  simply using the skeleton is still limited in enhancing the cycling stability of the anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 11, 27 and 28">(11,27,28)</a> The combination of carbon skeletons within different dimensions can not only elastically prevent the inflation and ensure the structural integrity but also immensely improve the conductivity and endow high-rate properties, which is an effective and general approach to improve the performance of conversion and alloying materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> Therefore, we believe that, if highly active heterojunctions are integrated with appropriately designed dual-carbon skeletons, a high-performance anode material and a novel composite structure for SIBs will be obtained.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this work, ZIF-67 was chosen as a precursor to construct the highly active CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO heterojunctions through the sulfidation of ZIF and partial reduction of carbon. Dual-carbon skeletons are designed to integrate internal open framework carbon-spheres (OFC) and external rGO nanomembrane encapsulating (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). By combing them together, a sandwich structure of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO is obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). When applied as the anode material for the SIB, benefitted from the special features and their synergistic effects, both the rate capability and cycling performance remarkably improved. The specific capacity can maintain ∼50% when the current density is increased by 200-fold from 0.05 to 10 A/g. Reversible capacities of 423 and 353 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> were achieved after 100 and 250 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, even after cycling for 3500 times at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it still maintains a reversible capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a Coulomb efficiency of 99.6%. The integration of dual-carbon design with heterojunction not only achieves a high-performance anode material for SIB but also provides important references for the preparation of other similar electrode mateirals.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Illustrations of 3D structures and synthesis procedures of sandwich and porous nanostructured spheres of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO and its sodiation/desodiation process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06551&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section/Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p">Most of the chemicals were purchased from Aladdin Reagents, Shanghai, China, including sodium chloroacetate (ClCH<sub>2</sub>COONa, 99%), methanol (CH<sub>4</sub>O, ≥ 99.5%), cobalt nitrate (Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, ≥ 99%), carbon disulfide (CS<sub>2</sub>, AR), 2-methylimidazole (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>6</sub>N<sub>2</sub>, ≥ 98%), polyvinylpyrrolidone ((C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>9</sub>NO)<sub><i>n</i></sub>, PVP, M.W. 1 300 000), ethylene imine polymer (CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>NH)<sub><i>n</i></sub>, M.W.600, 99%), 1-(3-(dimethylamino)propyl)-3-ethylcarbodiimide hydrochloride (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub>ClN<sub>3</sub>, 98.0%), and graphene oxide gel (GO, 1–3 wt %). Powder of sulfur (S<sub>8</sub>, ≥ 99.0%) was purchased from Sigma-Aldrich, Shanghai, China. Sodium-ion battery electrolyte (1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/PC = 1:1 vol% with 5.0%FEC) was purchased from Suzhou Duoduo Chemical Technology Co., Ltd, Suzhou, China. Conductive carbon black (ECP600JD) was commercially available from Lion Corporation, Tokyo, Japan. All the chemicals were used without further purification.</div><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO</h4><div id="sec2_1_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Preparation of Open-Framework Carbon Spheres (OFCs)</h5><div class="NLM_p last">First, the OFC was prepared by a one-step aerosol approach using an ultrasonic spray pyrolysis (USP) method. In brief, a humidifier operated at a frequency of 1.7 MHz was used to nebulize the precursor solution (sodium chloroacetate, ClCH<sub>2</sub>COONa, 1.5 mol/L) into fine droplets. The generated droplets were then carried away by the argon flow (1–3 L/min) into a furnace (500–900 °C). The black powders after the pyrolysis were collected in ethanol bubblers. After reaction, the final products were washed and centrifuged with ethanol (50 vol %) for three times and dried in a vacuum oven at 60 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Preparation of Aminated GO Aqueous Solution</h5><div class="NLM_p last">The aminated GO solution was converted from the commercially purchased GO gel. The GO gel (1–3 wt %, Aladdin) was prepared into a solution of 0.5 mg/mL, and then, 10 mg/mL polyethylene imine was added under a continuously ultrasonic agitation, and the pH value was adjusted to 8. After half an hour, 3 mg/mL 1-(3-(dimethylamino)propyl)-3-ethylcarbodiimide hydrochloride was added. The obtained liquid was stirred continuously for 24 h to obtain an aqueous solution of aminated GO.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_1_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Preparation of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO</h5><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical procedure, the prepared OFC of 10 mg was dispersed inside the methanol solution, followed by the successive addition of 100 mg of PVP, 0.5 mmol of cobalt nitrate, and 4 mmol of 2-methylimidazole. The mixed solution was stirred continuously at room temperature for 4 h to obtain a purple precursor of OFC@ZIF-67. During the following transformation synthesis process, the obtained OFC@ZIF-67 precursors and sulfur powders were placed in the rear and front of a tube furnace, respectively. The furnace was heated to 400 °C and held for 2 h in a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. After that, the obtained black powder was washed with reflux carbon disulfide (CS<sub>2</sub>) for 2 h to obtain OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO. A certain OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO was dispersed in water to form a concentration of 1 mg/mL. The same amount of preaminated GO with positive surface was added slowly under stirring, which made the GO well-capsulized onto the surface of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO. Finally, OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO was obtained after a calcination process at 400 °C for 2h in N<sub>2</sub> in a furnace.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_1_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Preparation of CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO</h5><div class="NLM_p last">CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO was prepared as the control group sample by the same procedure as OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO, except that the OFC was not added in the preparation process.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">2.2.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Morphologies and microstructures of the samples were characterized using a field emission scanning electron microscope (FE-SEM, Hitachi, S-4800) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEOL, JEM-2100EX). An EDS (Oxford, TN-5400) attached to the SEM was used to obtain the contents and distributions of the samples’ elements. Crystalline structures of the samples were analyzed on a powder X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ= 0.154056 nm) (XRD, Bruker, D8 Advance). An X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS, Kratos, AXIS Ultra DLD, Al Kα source) was used to determine chemical bonding information on elements. The obtained values of electron binding energy were calibrated according to the value of adventurous carbon (C 1s, 284.6 eV), and the data analysis was carried out using XPS Peak 41 software. A Raman spectrometer (Raman, inVia, Renishaw) and Fourier transform infrared spectrometer (FT-IR, Thermo Scientific, Nicolet iS10) were applied to identify the molecular structures and bonding modes of the samples. The surface area of the sample was obtained using a porosity analyzer (Micromeritics, Tristar 3020), and the adsorption isotherm was measured at −196 °C. The specific surface areas and pore diameter distributions of the samples were obtained by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) and BJH models, respectively. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was carried out on a STA 409 thermal analyzer with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in a flowing air atmosphere. A ζ potential analyzer (Litesizer 500) was used to obtain the surface potentials of samples.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical measurements were performed using the CR2016 coin-type cells assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. To prepare the working electrodes, active substance, conductive carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride binder with a mass ratio of 8:1:1 were mixed uniformly in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone to form a slurry. The obtained slurry was then uniformly coated onto a copper foil with a thickness of 150 μm and dried overnight in a vacuum oven at 70 °C. Subsequently, the coated copper foil was punched into wafers, and the mass loading on the wafer was 1.5 ± 0.1 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. Sodium metal and glass fiber were used as the counter electrode and separator, respectively. Galvanostatic electrochemical experiments were conducted using a multichannel battery test system (LAND CT2001A) at 25 °C. The cyclic voltammetry curves (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were carried out using a Princeton Applied Research VersaSTAT4 electrochemical workstation. The specific capacities of all the samples were calculated on the basis of the total mass of active materials.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.1.  Morphology and Structure</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a> shows the schematic views of fabrication and sodiation/desodiation processes of the hierarchically porous composites (OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO). First, the ZIF-67 dodecahedrons were decorated onto the OFC, which is prepared by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1 and S2, Supporting Information</a>). Then, the prepared OFC@ZIF-67 was <i>in situ</i> transformed onto OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO <i>via</i> the sulfurization and partial reduction process of carbon. Afterward, preaminated GO with its positive potential on the surface was employed to capsulize the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO through an electrostatic self-assembly process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Finally, the sample was annealed under N<sub>2</sub> to form OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO, which contains dual carbon skeletons and highly active CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO heterojunctions. The optimized sandwich structure is based on the stable rigid carbon skeleton with a hierarchically porous morphology (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4–S6</a>). Such a porous morphology can reduce the volume expansion effect, and facilitate mass transfer, thus enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage as the anode material.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> shows the morphologies and microstructures of the prepared samples. The internal 3D carbon skeleton is an open framework, having abundant cross-linked macropores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), which can provide abundant spaces for mass transfer and surface decoration. The smooth ZIF-67 dodecahedrons can be uniformly located on these OFCs with an average inner channel size of ∼200 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). After their <i>in situ</i> transformation, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO heterojunctions with mesoporous structures were formed, and they display similar morphologies to those of ZIF-67 except that their surfaces are much coarser (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d). The TEM image indicates that CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO structures are hollow and distorted dodecahedrons assembled by evenly distributed nanoparticles with an average size of ∼20 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). Such small building blocks can expose more surface atoms and provide higher activities for electrochemical reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> When they are further encapsulated with highly flexible and strong graphene membranes, the targeted product of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO is obtained, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f,g shows their SEM and TEM images. Its selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern exhibits diffraction rings, indicating their polycrystalline structure. These diffraction rings can be indexed to (200) and (210) planes of the CoS<sub>2</sub> crystal. A high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image exhibits lattice fringes with interplanar spacings of 2.45, 2.48, and 2.81 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h), which can be assigned to the (111) plane of the CoO crystal and (200) and (210) planes of the CoS<sub>2</sub> crystal, respectively. The TEM analysis results are consistent with those of SAED, demonstrating the formation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO. The elemental dot mapping with EDS analysis shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i demonstrates the existence of C, Co, and S elements, and they are evenly distributed inside the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphology characterization. (a–c) SEM images of OFC, OFC@ZIF-67, and OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, respectively; (d and e) TEM images in different magnifications of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, (f) SEM image, (g) TEM image, (h) HRTEM image with inset SAED pattern, and (i) elemental mapping with EDS of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06551&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a shows the XRD patterns of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, which display four major peaks at the positions 32.3°, 36.2°, 46.3°, and 54.9°, corresponding to the (200), (210), (220), and (311) crystal planes of CoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Two weak diffraction peaks can also be observed at 36.5° and 42.4° in the XRD pattern, which conforms to the (111) and (200) crystal planes of CoO, indicating the existence of CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO heterojunction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The diffraction peaks of OFC@ZIF-67 precursor disappear (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). This result is consisted with the XRD pattern of CoS<sub>2</sub> converted from ZIF-67 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7 and S8</a>). After OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO is capsulated with rGO, the XRD pattern shows a broad peak at ∼21.2°, which is corresponding to the diffraction peak of the (002) plane of graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> It is well-known that, in the Raman spectra of a carbon material, the D peak (1341 cm<sup>–1</sup>, standing for disorder) is the characteristic disordered carbon with structural defects, whereas the G peak (1587 cm<sup>–1</sup>, standing for graphite carbon) is associated with graphite carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> Their peak strength <i>I</i><sub>D</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>G</sub> are proportional to the contents of disordered and ordered carbon atoms, respectively. The obtained ratio values of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> for samples of OFC, OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, and OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO are 0.67, 0.98, and 0.92, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). This reveals that most of the carbon atoms exist in sp<sup>2</sup> type, which could enhance the electrical conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The synthesis process of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO can be confirmed by the FT-IR analysis. FT-IR spectrum of GO reveals the peaks with wave numbers of ∼3250 and ∼1650 cm<sup>–1</sup>, revealing the presence of −NH<sub>2</sub> and −COOH on the surface of GO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> High-temperature treatment under the N<sub>2</sub> results in significantly decreased intensities for these peaks, indicating that the GO has been reduced into rGO. To further understand the valence and bond information on OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO, XPS analysis was performed. The survey spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c) demonstrates the existence of C, Co, and S elements. The spectrum of C 1s can be divided into three peaks. The single peak at 284.6 eV is exclusively attributed to C═C bonds without any traces of other bonds, and higher BEs of 286.3 and 289.0 eV are corresponding to those of C─O/C─S and C═O, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> The Co 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e) can be deconvoluted into three pairs of peaks, i.e., Co─S bonds (782.1 and 798.8 eV), Co─O bonds mainly due to the partial reduction of OFC (778.8 and 793.5 eV), and satellite peaks of Co<sub>2p1/2</sub> and Co<sub>2p3/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The S 2p spectrum can be deconvoluted into two pairs at 169.0 and 170.1 and 165.1 and 163.9 eV, which are assigned to S<sup>2–</sup> and S─O bonds, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> All the XPS analysis results confirm the formation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO on OFC. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption test reveals that OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO has a specific surface area of 74.06 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> with an average pore diameter of ∼5.9 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). TG analysis under air shows that the mass contents of CoS<sub>2</sub>, CoO, and carbon in the composite are 30.45%, 4.55%, and 65%, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Such highly porous structures can facilitate effective diffusions of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the infiltration of electrolyte, which can enhance the electrochemical performance.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Structural characterization. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of the prepared samples; (c–f) full XPS and high-resolution XPS spectra of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06551&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> presents the electrochemical performance of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO as an anode material for SIBs. In all the tests, half coin cells with sodium metal as the counter electrode were used. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the initial three cycles show its operation potential window from 0 to 3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The first CV curve exhibits two irreversible reduction peaks at 1.0 and 0.6 V during its discharge process, but they disappear in the following two cycles, clearly indicating the formation of a layer of SEI film afterward. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> In the following two cycles, only the reduction peaks concentrated at ∼0.8 V appear during the discharge process, which are associated with <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="reactions 1">reactions 1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, while all the three CV cycles show the same oxidation peaks at ∼1.6 V, which is associated with <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="reaction 3">reaction 3</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i15"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i16"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i17"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties. (a) CV curves for the first three cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>  and (b) voltage profiles for the first three cycles and the 50th cycle at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO; (c and d) rate performance  and its charge/discharge cureves, (e) Nyquist plots, and (f) cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of the control and target samples; (g) long-term cycling performance of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO at 0.5 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 1000 and 3500 cycles, respectively (inset, SEM images of electrode materials before and after cycling).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06551&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Its CV curves after the first test overlap well, revealing the fast stability feature that benefits from its composite structure. Similar peak positions can also be observed in the results of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S12a and S13a</a>), demonstrating their similar electrochemical behaviors to the anode for SIBs. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO half cell tested at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. The GCD curves present one discharge plateau at 0.9 V and one charge plateau at 1.6 V, consistent with the positions of main reduction and oxidation peaks in the CV curves, respectively, whereas the second and third charge/discharge curves of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO are more consistent, which is dramatically different from those of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S12b and S13b</a>). These results demonstrate that OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO has the optimal structure to realize interfacial stability among these three samples, whereas the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO without being encapsulated by the rGO shows the worst stability performance. The results clearly manifest that the significantly improved cycling property is attributed to the layer of encapsulated rGO <i>via</i> stabilizing reaction interfaces and restricting the volume expansion. These result in a good rate performance and a stable reversible capacity of 205.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, even after the current density is increased up to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This value is much higher than those of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). When the current density is decreased to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO almost recovers to its initial value. The OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO electrode exhibites a characteristic voltage plateau when charging/discharging processes are performed under these currents (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d), suggesting that the structure of the electrode material has not been apparently damaged by the volume expansion effect during the rapid charge and discharge processes. Analysis of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO was further performed after activation. In a typical Nyquist diagram, the radius of the high-frequency semicircle reflects the scale of the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), and the slope of the oblique line in low-frequency region reflects the scale of the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The EIS curves of the activated and unactivated samples are fitted using two different models, and the results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14 and Table S1</a>) clearly show that the main barriers during electrochemical reactions are due the value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. Among all the samples, OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO has the smallest values of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e), indicating its excellent electron transfer and diffusion capability of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> When the OFC or rGO is absent, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of the other two materials are increased significantly, demonstrating that the transfer of electrons inside the materials becomes difficult. This may be the main reason for the poor discharge performance of the electrodes of OFC@ CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO. Results strongly confirm the important role of the dual carbon strategy for the improved electrochemcial performance. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f, OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO still delivers a reversible discharge capacity of 353 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 250 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> with a Coulomb efficiency of 98.3%. However, an obvious decline of capacity can be observed from the initial several cycles when either OFC or rGO is absent. Therefore, the synergistic effects generated from the dual carbon sources, together with contributions from a stable OFC and conductive rGO, are the key reasons for the stable cycling performance of active CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO. Therefore, the capacity of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO at a smaller current of 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> only shows a slight drop after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). Long-term cycling property is an important parameter to evaluate the performance of the electrode materials. Charge and discharge cycling tests were performed on OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO under high current densities of 0.5 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g. Even after 1000 cycles, a stable capacity of 229 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is still maintained at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the Coulomb efficiency is ∼99.5% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). Remarkably, the electrode can still maintain a stable capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after a long cycling for 3500 times at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; meanwhile, the structures remain stable without apparent damage (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g). By contrast, both the electrodes of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO show poor performance with obvious declines during their cycling tests. The specific capacity of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO is quickly attenuated to ∼60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>), and CoS<sub>2</sub>@rGO also shows poor stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>). All the above results demonstrate the superior performance of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO, which is attributed to the efficient internal support and electron transport provided by the OFC and the formation of stable interfaces due to the encapsulated rGO.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the underlying mechanisms of this significantly improved performance, the reaction kinetics were studied using the Dunn method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> For the CV curves, the relationship between current (<i>I</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) can be described using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>, and it can be further changed to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eq 5">eq 5</a> for the convenience of calculation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i18"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i19"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the fitting values. When the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, it indicates that the electrochemical process is controlled by the diffusion process, whereas when the <i>b</i> value is close to 1, the electrochemical reaction process is dominated by capacitive behavior. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> The CV curves were obtained at different scan rates from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and the relationship plots of the log (<i>i</i>) and log (<i>v</i>) were obtained using the main oxidation peak A and reduction peak B during the discharge/charge processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). The calculated <i>b</i> values for the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO are 0.7962 and 0.8581 for the reduction and oxidation processes, respectively, which indicates the obvious capacitance behavior during the discharge/charge processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a> The contribution ratios of capacitive behavior are further calculated using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="eqs 6">eqs 6</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq7" aria-label="7">7</a>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i20"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq7"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_m007.gif" alt="" id="_i21"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(7)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the contributions of capacitive and diffusion-controlled behaviors, respectively. The obtained contribution ratios of capacitive behavior (i.e., <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>/(<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>+ <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) are 61.0%, 65.9%, 72.9%, 77.8%, and 88%, respectively, at scan rates of 0.1–2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. The corresponding contributions of diffusion and capacitive behaviors at various scan rates are visually presented in blue and pink colors in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/suppl_file/am2c06551_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>. The large <i>b</i> values and significant capacitive contributions of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO reveal that the capacitive controlled behavior dominates the whole capacity, especially at high scan rates. This indicates that most redox reactions of Na<sup>+</sup> ions occur on the electrode surface, which enhances the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO to achieve faster charge storage and also a better rate performance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/medium/am2c06551_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Analysis of electrochemical principle of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO. (a) CV curves  at different scan rates; (b) relationship between log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>v</i>); (c) capacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution ratios at different scan rates; (d)  separation of capacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution areas at 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06551/asset/images/large/am2c06551_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06551&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">On the basis of the above analysis, we can summarize that the newly designed OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO composites have the following advantages to enhance the anode performances of SIBs:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(1)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The rigid OFC with run-through macropores offers good conductivity and enhances the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which are favorable for the pervasion of electrolyte and the delivery of electrons; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(2)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The mesoporous CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO not only has a good Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity but also effectively restricts the volume expansion and reduces the stress during the cycling processes, both of which are beneficial to enhancing the anode performance of the SIBs; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a></p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(3)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The rGOs are encapsulated strongly surrounding the OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO composite. They stabilize the active CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, and reduce the formation of excessive solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and loss of capacity decay. They also provide an excellent electron delivery route and promote the uniform distribution of electrons on the surface of the material. All of these facilitate a significantly enhanced rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></p></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i22">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, the sandwich structure of OFC@CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO@rGO is successfully fabricated by integrating dual carbon skeletons and highly active heterojunctions of CoS<sub>2</sub>/CoO, which significantly improves both specific capacity and cycling stability as the anode material for the SIB. Such structures have merits of high reaction activity, accelerated kinetics, and structure stability. As a result, it can deliver a high capacity of 353 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 250 cycles. Remarkably, even after 3500 times of cycling at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it can still maintain a reversible capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Such a design strategy can be effectively applied to the designs of other high-performance electrode materials for different types of rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ni- and/or Mn-based layered transition metal oxides as cathode materials for sodium ion batteries: status, challenges and countermeasures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Shenghan; Sun, Chenglin; Wang, Ning; Zhang, Qichun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been attracting great interest as alternatives for grid and mobile energy storage applications in the modern world due to the sufficient natural abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  To accelerate the practical application of SIB systems, the electrochem. performance of cathode materials should be further improved to satisfy the increasing worldwide demand.  As promising cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs) exhibit high specific capacity and high energy d. due to their appropriate voltage window.  Here, recent progress and achievements of three representative LTMOs including Ni-based, Mn-based, and Ni/Mn co-based cathodes in terms of the relationship between structural design and electrochem. performance are summarized, aiming at cost redn. and performance improvement.  The insights in this review focus on the development of LTMOs as cathodes for SIBs including overall battery performance, prepn. strategies and operation mechanism, therefore speeding up the commercialization of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzyYSXXlN_D7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj_lX9LL2Uwag"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXlvFemtb4%253D&amp;md5=cffd0e4e9b3e89477f2ab6eba082395f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1007/s12209-021-00281-z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Development of Metal and Metal-Based Composites Anode Materials for Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Transactions of Tianjin University</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>27</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">3</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">248</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">268</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review abstr.: Potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) are considered the next powerful potential generation energy storage system because of substantial potassium resource availability and similar characteristics with lithium.  Unfortunately, the actual application of KIBs is inferior to that of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), in which the finite energy d., ordinary circular life, and underdeveloped fabrication technique dominate the key constraints.  Various works have recently been directed to growing novel anode electrodes with superior electrochem. capability.  Noticeably, metals/metal oxides materials (e.g., Sb, Sn, Zn, SnO2, and MoO2) have been widely investigated as KIBs anodes because of high theor. capacity, suggesting outstanding promise for high-energy KIBs.  In this review, the latest research of metals/metal oxides electrodes for potassium storage is summarized.  The major strategies to control the electrochem. property of metals/metal oxides electrodes are discussed.  Finally, the future investigation foreground for these anode electrodes has been proposed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQZYbF8A46wLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljXDhKY2fNtUA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhtFKjsbrF&amp;md5=dc36dcc76edd920815584cd3a924daa8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1007/s41918-018-0008-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Advances in Sodium-Ion Battery Materials</span>. <i>Electrochemical Energy Reviews</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">3</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">294</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">323</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Sodium-Ion Battery Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongjin; Xiao, Lifen; Chen, Zhongxue; Ai, Xinping; Cao, Yuliang; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Grid-scale energy storage systems with low-cost and high-performance electrodes are needed to meet the requirements of sustainable energy systems.  Due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources and their similar electrochem. to the established lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as ideal candidates for grid-scale energy storage systems.  In the past decade, though tremendous efforts have been made to promote the development of SIBs, and significant advances have been achieved, further improvements are still required in terms of energy/power d. and long cyclic stability for commercialization.  In this review, the latest progress in electrode materials for SIBs, including a variety of promising cathodes and anodes, is briefly summarized.  Besides, the sodium storage mechanisms, endeavors on electrochem. property enhancements, structural and compositional optimizations, challenges and perspectives of the electrode materials for SIBs are discussed.  Though enormous challenges may lie ahead, we believe that through intensive research efforts, sodium-ion batteries with low operation cost and longevity will be commercialized for large-scale energy storage application in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoo58FwhtNvWbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljXDhKY2fNtUA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">3D spongy CoS2 nanoparticles/carbon composite as high-performance anode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yaohui; Wang, Nana; Sun, Changhui; Lu, Zhenxiao; Xue, Pan; Tang, Bin; Bai, Zhongchao; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">332</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A spongy CoS2/carbon composite assembled from CoS2 nanoparticles (∼20 nm) homogeneously anchored on a spongy carbon matrix was synthesized through a facile freeze-drying method and a hydrothermal process.  As anode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs), this composite shows significantly enhanced lithium/sodium storage performance with the synergetic effects due to the elec. cond. of the carbon matrix and the porous structure, which provide buffer spaces for vol. expansion during charge/discharge processes and feasible transfer pathways for electrons/ions.  The electrochem. results demonstrate that the spongy CoS2/carbon composite is an outstanding anode material for LIBs and SIBs.  It delivers a high specific capacity of 610 mAh g-1 at 500 mA g-1 after 120 cycles in LIBs and 330 mAh g-1 at 500 mA g-1 after 60 cycles in SIBs, resp.  Moreover, the freeze-drying/hydrothermal process developed in this work could be useful for the construction of many other high-capacity metal sulfide composites as electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwXdfLzDrWz7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWj_OiYF6Yww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhsFGntr3O&amp;md5=bec339669bfe46b9b745e69b715fe77d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201801879"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Progress and Challenges</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">133</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">144</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Progress and Challenges</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Biwei; Rojo, Teofilo; Li, Xiaolin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemSusChem</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Hard C (HC) is the state-of-the-art anode material for Na-ion batteries due to its excellent overall performance, wide availability, and relatively low cost.  Recently, tremendous effort has been invested to elucidate the sodium storage mechanism in HC, and to explore synthetic approaches that can enhance the performance and lower the cost.  However, disagreements remain in the field, particularly on the fundamental questions of ion transfer and storage and the ideal HC structure for high performance.  This paper provides an anal. and summary of the theor. limitations of HC, discrepancies in the storage mechanism, and methods to improve the performance.  Future research on developing ideal structured HCs, advanced electrolytes, and optimized electrolyte-electrode interphases are proposed on the basis of recent progress.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpc09euZGW0wbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWj_OiYF6Yww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit1Oqu7jE&amp;md5=c493076faac0ea6afd3bc0c7a34a1810</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of carbon materials and their composites with alloy metals for sodium ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Balogun, Muhammad-Sadeeq; Luo, Yang; Qiu, Weitao; Liu, Peng; Tong, Yexiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">98</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are currently emerging as a replacement for lithium ion batteries due to sodium abundance in the earth's crust.  The recent development of sodium ion batteries anode has been achieved by the use of selected carbon as an anode.  The performance of the selected carbon materials such as the charge/discharge capacity, voltage profile and cyclic stability, depend strongly on the sizes, additives, electrolytes and structures.  Based on the contribution of the carbon-based materials as a replacement of the sodium ion batteries anode, much improvement has been achieved.  However, more investigations are still required in order to identify the suitable carbon materials that can provide and improve the anode properties.  In the same vein, alloy materials have gained increasing effort as high capacity anode and their combination with carbon have contributed to the development of sodium ion batteries.  In this review, we have accounted for the development of carbon-based materials and their composites with alloy materials as well as their prospects and challenges for sodium ion battery anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqLqTy97T_9sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhKUweXJtWbhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVOmsbvM&amp;md5=5bc4c118e6be905d663496bf2953e8d7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703238"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Energy Storage Mechanism, Challenge and Design Strategies of Metal Sulfides for Rechargeable Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Qingguang; Tong, Zhaopeng; Su, Yuanqiang; Qin, Sheng; Tang, Yongbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2103912</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable sodium/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs) with abundant reserves of Na/K and low cost have been a promising substitution to com. lithium-ion batteries.  As for pivotal anode materials, metal sulfides (MSx) exhibit an inspiring potential due to the multitudinous redox storage mechanisms for SIBs/PIBs applications.  Nevertheless, they still confront several bottlenecks, such as the low elec. cond., poor ionic diffusivity, sluggish interfacial/surface reaction kinetics, and severe vol. expansion, which distinctly restrain the battery performance.  Meanwhile, the systematic insights into the design strategies of MSx for SIBs/PIBs have been seldom elaborated.  In this review, the energy storage mechanism, challenge, and design strategies of MSx for SIBs/PIBs are expounded to address the above predicaments.  In particular, design strategies of MSx are highlighted from the aspects of morphol. modifications involving 1D/2D/3D configurations, at.-level engineering contg. heteroatom doping, vacancy creation, and interlayer spacing expansion, and MSx composites with other MSx, metal oxides, carbonaceous, and graphite materials to boost the comprehensive electrochem. performance of SIBs/PIBs.  Furthermore, prospects are presented for the further advance of MSx to surmount imminent challenges, hoping to forecast feasible future orientations in this field.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqoOBzu8A-7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh65v4LY2ZZgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhsVentb3I&amp;md5=fe1e503a51a1a89e315e648570f0d311</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001331"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Temperature Shock Enabled Nanomanufacturing for Energy-Related Applications</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Temperature Shock Enabled Nanomanufacturing for Energy-Related Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Shuming; Xu, Jie; Cui, Xiaoya; Liu, Weidi; Zhang, Zhicheng; Deng, Yida; Hu, Wenbin; Chen, Yanan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review,.  Functional nanomaterials are playing a crucial role in the emerging field of energy-related devices.  Recently, as a novel synthesis method, high-temp. shock (HTS), which is rapid, low cost, eco-friendly, universal, scalable, and controllable, has provided a promising option for the rational design and synthesis of various high-quality nanomaterials.  In this report, the HTS technique, including the equipment setup and operating principle, is systematically introduced, and recent progress in the synthesis of nanomaterials for energy storage and conversion applications using this HTS method is summarized.  The growth mechanisms of nanoparticles and carbonaceous nanomaterials are thoroughly discussed, followed by the summary of the characteristic advantages of the HTS strategy.  A series of nanomaterials prepd. by the HTS method, including carbon-based films, metal nanoparticles and compd. nanoparticles, show high performance in the diverse applications of storage energy batteries, highly active catalysts, and smart energy devices.  Finally, the future perspectives and directions of HTS in nanomanufg. for broader applications are presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpTrIeDlwbLIrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh65v4LY2ZZgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsVWgtbjO&amp;md5=132c55a8221fc4b1e78f465ac826a760</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA00833G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Assembly and Superior Sodium Storage Properties of a Sea-Sponge Structured C/SnS@C Nanocomposite</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem.
